The NAIC is the organization of all state insurance regulators and provides tools for consumers to report issues. Consumer Financial Protection Bureau (CFPB) â€“ A federal agency that oversees consumer financial products and services: banks, credit reporting, debt collection, loans, and also some insurance-adjacent products. If your campaign touches on financial or credit abuses (for instance, predatory student loans, improper hospital billing affecting credit, or credit-reporting issues due to institutional failures), CFPB can help. They take consumer complaints and have authority to enforce against unfair, deceptive, or abusive practices.
Contact: Submit a Complaint via the CFPB website (consumerfinance.gov/complaint). There is also a phone line at 1-855-411-CFPB (2372) for questions and a TTY/TDD line (202-435-9742). The CFPBâ€™s main office is 1700 G Street NW, Washington, DC 20552, but use electronic submission for speed.
Submission Portal: CFPB Complaint Portal â€“ Choose the category of your issue (e.g. â€œDebt collectionâ€ or â€œCredit reportingâ€ or â€œBank accountâ€) and fill out the form. CFPB will forward your complaint to the company in question and require a response. You can monitor the status online and will get a response typically within 30-60 days. Notably, CFPB also publishes a public Consumer Complaint Database (with personal details redacted) â€“ this can shine a light on patterns.
Notes: Use CFPB if the systemic corruption youâ€™re addressing has a financial consumer angle. For example, if foster youth had their credit ruined due to agency mismanagement, or a telecom company charged unlawful fees, or an insurance company harmed consumers financially â€“ those can fall under CFPB if framed appropriately. The CFPB is relatively responsive and has taken actions based on complaint data (for instance, going after a student loan servicer after receiving many complaints). Even if itâ€™s not directly within CFPBâ€™s purview, they might forward it or use it to coordinate with other regulators. Because your project is broad, consider CFPB as one tool for any economic injustice components of the story.
Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC) â€“ Office of the Whistleblower â€“ If the corporate misconduct involves securities fraud, investor deception, or bribery of officials (FCPA violations), the SECâ€™s whistleblower program might be relevant. The SEC rewards eligible whistleblowers who provide original information leading to successful enforcement actions for violations of U.S. securities laws. For example, if a public company involved in your story lied in its disclosures or a telecom firm falsified earnings or a tech company misled investors about an AI product, those would be SEC matters.
Contact: Submit a tip through the SECâ€™s online Tips, Complaints and Referrals (TCR) portal (accessible at sec.gov). You can also file Form TCR by mail or fax if needed. The SEC Whistleblower Office can be reached via email (whistleblower@sec.gov) for questions, but they cannot accept tips over email â€“ it must be via the official form. Thereâ€™s also a hotline for questions: (202) 551-4790.
Submission Portal: SEC TCR Electronic Form â€“ found on the SEC website under Enforcement > Whistleblower > Submit a Tip. You will need to provide as much detail as possible about the alleged fraud, including who is involved, how it violates securities laws, and any supporting evidence. You have the option to submit anonymously (but to be eligible for an award, youâ€™ll need an attorney to file on your behalf or reveal your identity if an award is considered).
Notes: This is a specialized channel â€“ use it if your information is specifically about fraud against investors or markets. The SEC is required by law to keep whistleblower identities confidential. If your tip leads to enforcement with over $1 million in penalties, you could receive 10-30% of the sanction as a reward. Even beyond monetary awards, the SEC route is a way to trigger investigations into corporate corruption that affects shareholders or markets. (For example, if a hospital corporation is publicly traded and you suspect it committed accounting fraud to hide losses from malpractice cases, that could be an SEC issue). The process can be slow and you might not hear updates due to confidentiality, but it can have serious impact. The Commodity Futures Trading Commission (CFTC) has a similar program for fraud in commodities, swaps, or crypto markets.
(For other industries: consider the Department of Justice (DOJ) Antitrust Division for systemic anti-competitive conduct, or the Federal Communications Commission (FCC) as noted, or specific federal agenciesâ€™ Inspectors General if federal contractors are involved in misconduct. Also, Better Business Bureau (BBB) can be used to lodge a public complaint about a specific company, which at least creates a record and sometimes pressures a response, though BBB is not a regulator. In sum, choose the oversight body that matches the sector of the wrongdoing.)
Medical & Mental Health Oversight
The Joint Commission â€“ An independent nonprofit that accredits and certifies health care organizations nationwide. Focus/Mission: Ensuring healthcare organizations provide safe, high-quality care. They investigate patterns that violate their standards (e.g. environment of care problems, patient rights abuses, inadequate infection control, etc.). They will not address billing issues or personnel disputes, but will address safety and quality concerns.

HHS Office of Inspector General (OIG) â€“ The OIG for HHS investigates fraud, waste, and abuse in HHS programs (Medicare, Medicaid, hospital grants, etc.). Media and Public Advocacy â€“ While not â€œorganizationsâ€ per se, itâ€™s worth noting that exposure through media is a form of oversight when formal channels fail.
